<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">In December&nbsp;2002, the Financial Accounting Standards Board (FASB)&nbsp;issued SFAS
No.&nbsp;148, &#147;Accounting for Stock-Based Compensation-Transition and Disclosure&#148;.
SFAS No.&nbsp;148 amends the transition and disclosure provisions of SFAS No.&nbsp;123.
SFAS No.&nbsp;148 allowed companies adopting the fair value based method of SFAS No.
123 to utilize one of three alternative transition methods as long as SFAS No.
123 was adopted in a fiscal year beginning prior to December&nbsp;16, 2003. To
date, the Company has not adopted SFAS No.&nbsp;123 and would therefore be required
to use one of two methods proscribed by SFAS No.&nbsp;148 if it adopted SFAS No.&nbsp;123
in the future. The FASB currently is working on a project to develop a new
standard for accounting for stock-based compensation and expects to issue a
final standard in the second half of 2004.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt"><I>Derivative Financial Instruments</I>


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">The Company records all derivative instruments on the balance sheet at their
fair value. Changes in the fair value of derivatives are recorded each period
in earnings or other comprehensive income, depending on the type of hedging
instrument and the effectiveness of the hedges.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">Operating results can be significantly impacted by changes in the price of
aircraft fuel. To manage the risks associated with changes in aircraft fuel
prices, the Company uses swap agreements and call options for crude oil and
other commodities. These contracts, referred to as &#147;fuel hedge



<P align="center" style="font-size: 10pt">64
</DIV>

<!-- PAGEBREAK -->
<P><HR noshade><P>
<H5 align="left" style="page-break-before:always"><A HREF="#tocpage">Table of Contents</A></H5><P>

<DIV style="font-family: 'Times New Roman',Times,serif">



<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">contracts,&#148;
have a high correlation to changes in aircraft fuel prices, and therefore
qualify as
cash flow hedges. Each period, the contracts are adjusted to fair market value.
The change in the value of the fuel hedge contracts that perfectly offsets the
change in the value of the aircraft fuel being hedged is recorded as other
comprehensive income or loss until the hedged contract is settled and is then
recognized in earnings as part of fuel expense. To the extent the change in
the value of the fuel hedge contracts does not perfectly offset the change in
the value of the aircraft fuel purchase being hedged, the change is recognized
in earnings as part of nonoperating income (expense).


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">At December&nbsp;31, 2003, the Company has fuel hedge contracts in place to hedge
132.1&nbsp;million gallons of its expected jet fuel usage in 2004, 116.1&nbsp;million
gallons in 2005 and 10.2&nbsp;million gallons in 2006. This represents 33%, 28% and
2% of the anticipated fuel consumption in 2004, 2005 and 2006, respectively.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">The Company recognized $7.4&nbsp;million in nonoperating expense in 2001, $6.4
million in nonoperating income in 2002 and $1.7&nbsp;million in nonoperating expense
in 2003 related to the ineffectiveness on fuel hedge contracts.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">As of December&nbsp;31, 2002 and 2003, the fair value of the Company&#146;s fuel hedge
positions were $17.4&nbsp;million and $18.4&nbsp;million, respectively. All of the 2002
fair value amounts and $12.0&nbsp;million of the 2003 fair value amounts are
included in prepaid expenses and other current assets in the consolidated
balance sheets. The remaining 2003 fair value amount of $6.4&nbsp;million is
reflected in other assets in the consolidated balance sheets.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">During 2002 and 2003, the Company recognized gains of $12.1&nbsp;million and $29.4
million, respectively, from the cash settlement of hedging activities. In
2001, hedging gains resulting from cash settlements of hedging activities were
de minimis. These gains are reflected in aircraft fuel in the consolidated
statements of operations.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">The Company enters into foreign exchange forward contracts, generally with
maturities of less than one month, to manage the risk associated with net
foreign currency transactions. Resulting gains and losses are recognized
currently in other operating expense. The Company periodically enters into
interest rate swap agreements to hedge interest rate risk. At December&nbsp;31,
2003, there were no foreign currency contracts or interest rate swap agreements
outstanding.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt"><I>New Accounting Standards</I>


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">In 2003, the Company adopted the accounting provisions of FASB Interpretation
No.&nbsp;45, &#147;Guarantor&#146;s Accounting and Disclosure Requirements for Guarantees,
Including Indirect Guarantees of Indebtedness of Others&#148; (&#147;FIN 45&#148;). FIN 45
requires a guarantor to recognize, at the inception of a guarantee, a liability
for the fair value of the obligation undertaken in issuing the guarantee. This
interpretation applies to guarantees issued or modified after December&nbsp;31,
2002. While the Company has various guarantees included in contracts in the
normal course of business, primarily in the form of indemnities, these
guarantees would only result in immaterial increases in future costs, and do
not represent significant commitments or contingent liabilities of the
indebtedness of others.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">In January&nbsp;2003, the FASB issued Interpretation No.&nbsp;46 (FIN 46), &#147;Consolidation
of Variable Interest Entities.&#148; FIN 46, as amended by FIN 46 (Revised) in
December&nbsp;2003, requires consolidation of



<P align="center" style="font-size: 10pt">65
</DIV>

<!-- PAGEBREAK -->
<P><HR noshade><P>
<H5 align="left" style="page-break-before:always"><A HREF="#tocpage">Table of Contents</A></H5><P>

<DIV style="font-family: 'Times New Roman',Times,serif">



<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">certain types of entities in which the
Company absorbs a majority of the entity&#146;s expected losses, receives a majority
of the entity&#146;s expected returns, or both, as a result
of ownership, contractual or other financial interests in the entity. These
entities are called &#147;variable interest entities.&#148; The principal characteristics
of variable interest entities are (1)&nbsp;an insufficient amount of equity to
absorb the entity&#146;s expected losses, (2)&nbsp;equity owners as a group are not able
to make decisions about the entity&#146;s activities, and (3)&nbsp;equity that does not
absorb the entity&#146;s losses or receive the entity&#146;s residual returns. Variable
interests are contractual, ownership or other monetary interests in an entity
that change with fluctuations in the entity&#146;s net asset value. As a result,
variable interest entities can arise from items such as lease agreements, loan
arrangements, guarantees or service contracts.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">If an entity is determined to be a variable interest entity, the entity must be
consolidated by the &#147;primary beneficiary&#148;. The primary beneficiary is the
holder of the variable interests that absorbs a majority of the variable
interest entity&#146;s expected losses or receives a majority of the entity&#146;s
residual returns in the event no holder has a majority of the expected losses.
There is no primary beneficiary in cases where no single holder absorbs the
majority of the expected losses or receives a majority of the residual returns.
The determination of the primary beneficiary is based on qualitative aspects
of the arrangements involved or projected cash flows at the inception of the
transaction with the variable interests entity.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">FIN 46 is applicable to financial statements of companies that have interests
in &#147;special purpose entities&#148;, as defined, during the year ended December&nbsp;31,
2003.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">The Company is the lessee in a series of operating leases covering the
Company&#146;s leased aircraft. In many instances, the lessors are trusts
established by a third party specifically to purchase, finance and lease
aircraft to the Company. These leasing entities meet the criteria for variable
interest entities. However, because the Company is not the primary beneficiary
of the entity&#146;s expected gains or losses, the Company is not required to
consolidate these entities. The leasing arrangements involved contain terms
consistent with market terms at their inception and do not include residual
value guarantees made by us, fixed-price purchase obligations or similar
features that would obligate the Company to absorb the majority of expected
losses of the variable interest entities. The Company&#146;s maximum expected
exposure under these types of lease arrangements is the remaining lease
payments, which are reflected in future lease commitments in Note 5 (plus the
cost, if any, of bringing the equipment into compliance with the physical
condition required for return).


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt"><I>Fourth Quarter Adjustments</I>


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">Operating loss for the three months and year ended December&nbsp;31, 2003 includes
adjustments to increase operating expenses by $2.8&nbsp;million (pretax)&nbsp;related to
prior years. Operating loss for the three months ended December&nbsp;31, 2003 also
includes adjustments to increase operating expenses by $1.8&nbsp;million (pretax)
and increase operating revenues by $6.3&nbsp;million (pretax), both related to
previous quarters in 2003. Management does not believe that these amounts are
material to the periods affected.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt"><B>Note 2. Marketable Securities</B>


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">At December&nbsp;31, 2002 and 2003 all of the Company&#146;s marketable securities were
classified as available-for-sale. The securities are carried at fair value,
with the unrealized



<P align="center" style="font-size: 10pt">66
</DIV>

<!-- PAGEBREAK -->
<P><HR noshade><P>
<H5 align="left" style="page-break-before:always"><A HREF="#tocpage">Table of Contents</A></H5><P>

<DIV style="font-family: 'Times New Roman',Times,serif">



<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">gains and losses reported in stockholders&#146; equity under the
caption &#147;Accumulated Other Comprehensive Income (Loss)&#148;. Realized gains and
losses are included in other nonoperating income (expense)&nbsp;in the consolidated
statements of operations. The cost of securities sold is based on the specific
identification method. Interest and dividends on marketable securities are
included in interest income in the consolidated statements of operations.

<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">*The weighted average fixed interest rate was 7.4% during 2002 and 2003. The
weighted average variable interest rate was 2.5% during 2002 and 2003,
respectively.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">At December&nbsp;31, 2003, borrowings of $955.1&nbsp;million were secured by flight
equipment and real property.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">On March&nbsp;21, 2003, the Company completed the private placement of $150.0
million of floating rate senior convertible notes due in 2023 (the Notes). The
Notes bear interest for the first five years from date of issuance at a
variable interest rate of 3-month LIBOR plus 2.5% (3.65% at December&nbsp;31, 2003).
This interest is paid quarterly in arrears. Thereafter, the Notes will cease
bearing cash interest and instead, the principal value of the Notes will
increase daily by the unpaid interest, which will be calculated at LIBOR plus
2.5%, up to a maximum of 5.25%.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">The Notes are convertible into shares of the Company&#146;s common stock at the
option of the holder at a price equal to the original or variable principal,
divided by 38.4615. At date of issuance, the conversion price was equal to
$26.00 per share. Upon conversion, the Company may deliver, in lieu of common
stock, cash or a combination of cash and common stock. The Company may



<P align="center" style="font-size: 10pt">67
</DIV>

<P><HR noshade><P>
<H5 align="left" style="page-break-before:always"><A HREF="#tocpage">Table of Contents</A></H5><P>

<DIV style="font-family: 'Times New Roman',Times,serif">



<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">redeem
all or a portion of the Notes in cash or common stock or a combination of cash
and common stock at any time on or after the third anniversary of the issuance
of the Notes. In addition, holders may require the Company to purchase all or
a portion of their Notes on the 5th, 10th and 15th anniversaries
of the issuance of the Notes or upon the occurrence of a change of control or
tax event at principal plus accrued interest.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">The Notes are senior unsecured obligations and rank equally with the Company&#146;s
existing and future senior unsecured indebtedness.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">Net proceeds from the offering totaled $144.9&nbsp;million. Approximately $18.4
million of the net proceeds is restricted and intended to fund interest
payments for the first three years and is reported as restricted cash ($7.2
million recorded in prepaid expenses and other current assets and $11.2&nbsp;million
in other assets) in the Company&#146;s Consolidated Balance Sheet as of December&nbsp;31,
2003. The remaining net proceeds from the offering are being used for working
capital requirements and general corporate purposes


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">During 2003, Alaska issued $129.5&nbsp;million of debt secured by flight equipment,
having interest rates that vary with LIBOR and payment terms of 12 to 15&nbsp;years.
In September&nbsp;2001, Alaska borrowed $150&nbsp;million under its credit facility at
an interest rate that varies with LIBOR and is payable on or before December
31, 2004.

<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt"><B>Note 5. Commitments</B>


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt"><I>Lease Commitments</I>


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">At December&nbsp;31, 2003, the Company had lease contracts for 113 aircraft that
have remaining noncancelable lease terms of one to 16&nbsp;years. The majority of
airport and terminal facilities are also leased. Total rent expense was $254.0
million, $274.1&nbsp;million and $288.1&nbsp;million, in 2001, 2002, and 2003,
respectively.

<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">* The 2004 capital lease obligation primarily represents the end of lease
buyout of two airplanes totaling $6.2&nbsp;million. In January&nbsp;2004, these leases
were renegotiated and will be reflected as operating leases in future periods.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt"><I>Aircraft Commitments</I>


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">The Company has firm orders for three Boeing 737 series aircraft to be
delivered between 2004 and 2005, two Bombardier Q400 aircraft to be delivered
in 2004, and ten Bombardier CRJ700 aircraft between 2004 and 2009. The firm
orders require payments of approximately $361&nbsp;million between 2004 and 2009.
As of December&nbsp;31, 2003, deposits of $72.3&nbsp;million related to the firm orders
had been made. In addition to the ordered aircraft, the Company has purchase
options on 26 Boeing 737s, 15 Q400s, and 25 CRJ700s.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">Alaska is party to a purchase agreement with an aircraft manufacturer. Under
the agreement, upon commitment to purchase the aircraft, Alaska must make
deposits to the manufacturer of a portion of the purchase price of the
aircraft, with the remainder of the purchase price due at delivery. Concurrent
with this agreement, the manufacturer has an agreement with a Trust wherein the
Trust makes deposits to the manufacturer of additional pre-delivery deposits
for the aircraft. Under certain specified events, including default by the
Trust, Alaska may be required to pay the manufacturer the amounts paid by the
Trust in order to retain the right to purchase the aircraft. Alaska makes
monthly payments to the Trust related to the advances made by the Trust to the
manufacturer, which payments are capitalized as part of the aircraft cost. The
Trust is reimbursed for its advances on or before the delivery of the
applicable aircraft. As of December&nbsp;31, 2003, Alaska&#146;s obligations related to
such advances equaled approximately $11.6&nbsp;million, which is scheduled for
payment during the first quarter of 2004.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt"><B>Note 6. Stock Plans</B>


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">The Company has three stock option plans that provide for the purchase of Air
Group common stock at stipulated prices on the dates of the grant by certain
officers and key employees of Air Group and its subsidiaries. Under the 1996,
1997, and 1999 Plans, options for 4,258,500 shares have been granted and, at
December&nbsp;31, 2003, 448,400 shares were available for grant. Under all plans,
the stock options granted have terms of up to ten years. Substantially all
grantees are 25% vested after one year, 50% after two years, 75% after three
years, and 100% after four years.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">The Company follows the intrinsic value method prescribed by APB Opinion No.&nbsp;25
and related Interpretations in accounting for stock options. Accordingly, no
compensation cost has been recognized for these plans as




<P align="center" style="font-size: 10pt">69
</DIV>

<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">In August&nbsp;2002, the Company adopted an Employee Stock Purchase Plan (the ESPP
Plan) which is intended to qualify under Section&nbsp;423 of the Internal Revenue
Code. Under the terms of the ESPP Plan, employees can purchase Company common
stock at 85% of the lower of the fair market value on the first or the last day
of each quarterly offering period. Proceeds received from the issuance of
shares are credited to stockholders&#146; equity in the period in which the shares
are issued. In 2002 and 2003, 24,157 shares and 135,043 shares, respectively,
were purchased by Company employees under the ESPP Plan.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt"><B>Note 7. Employee Benefit Plans</B>


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">Four defined benefit and five defined contribution retirement plans cover
various employee groups of Alaska and Horizon. The defined benefit plans
provide benefits based on an employee&#146;s term of service and average
compensation for a specified period of time before retirement. Alaska and
Horizon also maintain unfunded, noncontributory defined benefit plans for
certain elected officers. A summary of each plan follows:


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt"><I>Pension Plans-Defined Benefit</I>


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">The Company&#146;s pension plans are funded as required by the Employee Retirement
Income Security Act of 1974 (ERISA). The defined benefit plan assets consist
primarily of marketable equity and fixed income securities. The Company uses a
December&nbsp;31 measurement date for these plans. The following table sets forth
the status of the plans for 2002 and 2003 (in millions):

<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">In determining the expected return on plan assets, the Company assesses the
current level of expected returns on risk free investments (primarily
government bonds), the historical level of the risk premium associated with the
other asset classes in which the portfolio is invested and the expectations for
future returns of each asset class. The expected return for each asset class
is then weighted based on the target asset allocation to develop the expected
long-term rate of return on assets assumption for the portfolio.

<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">The Company&#146;s investment policy focuses on achieving maximum returns at a
reasonable risk for pension assets over a full market cycle. The Company uses
a number of fund managers and invests in various asset classes to diversify
risk. Target allocations for the primary asset classes are
approximately:


<DIV align="center">
<TABLE style="font-size: 10pt" cellspacing="0" border="0" cellpadding="0" width="55%">

</TR>


<!-- End Table Body -->
</TABLE>
</DIV>


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">Pension assets are rebalanced periodically to maintain the above target asset
allocations. Up to 12% of the equity portfolio may be invested in a pooled
fund of small capitalization stocks. An individual equity investment will not
exceed 10% of the entire equity portfolio. Fixed income securities carry a
minimum &#147;A&#148; rating by Moody&#146;s and/or Standard and Poor&#146;s and the average life
of the bond portfolio may not exceed 10&nbsp;years.

<P><HR noshade><P>
<H5 align="left" style="page-break-before:always"><A HREF="#tocpage">Table of Contents</A></H5><P>

<P align="left"><FONT size="2">In 2002, the Company recorded an $87.2&nbsp;million (net of taxes of $52.5&nbsp;million)
non-cash charge to equity in connection with the defined benefit plans that the
Company sponsors for eligible employees. This charge resulted from an unfunded
accrued benefit obligation resulting from lower than expected returns on plan
assets and a reduction in discount rate. In 2003, the Company recorded a
reduction of this equity charge of $8.5&nbsp;million ($5.2&nbsp;million, net of taxes of
$3.3&nbsp;million) primarily reflecting higher than expected return on assets.
</FONT>
<P align="left"><FONT size="2">The Company expects to contribute $49.0&nbsp;million to these defined benefit
pension plans during 2004.
</FONT>
<P align="left"><FONT size="2"><I>Pension Plans-Noncontributory</I>
</FONT>

<P align="left"><FONT size="2"><I>Defined Contribution Plans</I>
</FONT>
<P align="left"><FONT size="2">The defined contribution plans are deferred compensation plans under section
401(k) of the Internal Revenue Code. All of these plans require Company
contributions. Total expense for the defined contribution plans was $19.0
million, $20.6&nbsp;million, and $21.7&nbsp;million, respectively, in 2001, 2002, and
2003.
</FONT>
<P align="center"><FONT size="2">72</FONT>
<!-- PAGEBREAK -->
<P><HR noshade><P>
<H5 align="left" style="page-break-before:always"><A HREF="#tocpage">Table of Contents</A></H5><P>
<P align="left"><FONT size="2"><I>Profit Sharing Plans</I>
</FONT>
<P align="left"><FONT size="2">Alaska and Horizon have employee profit sharing plans. There was no profit
sharing expense during 2001 or 2002. In 2003, the Company recorded profit
sharing expense of $3.7&nbsp;million ($2.9&nbsp;million Alaska and $0.8&nbsp;million Horizon).
</FONT>
<P align="left"><FONT size="2"><I>Other Postretirement Benefits</I>
</FONT>
<P align="left"><FONT size="2">The Company allows retirees to continue their medical, dental, and vision
benefits by paying all or a portion of the active employee plan premium until
eligible for Medicare, currently age 65. This results in a subsidy to
retirees, because the premiums received by the Company are less than the actual
cost of the retirees&#146; claims. The accumulated postretirement benefit
obligation (APBO)&nbsp;for this subsidy is unfunded, and at December&nbsp;31, 2002 and
2003 was $52.7&nbsp;million and $77.3&nbsp;million, respectively. This liability was
determined using an assumed discount rate of 6.75% and 6.00% in 2002 and 2003,
respectively. The accrued liability related to the subsidy is included with
other liabilities on the Consolidated Balance Sheet, and totaled $31.3&nbsp;million
and $39.2&nbsp;million at December&nbsp;31, 2002 and 2003, respectively. Annual expense
related to this subsidy was approximately $4.6&nbsp;million in 2001, $4.4&nbsp;million in
2002, and $9.6&nbsp;million in 2003.
</FONT>
<P align="left"><FONT size="2">The Company uses a December&nbsp;31 measurement date to assess obligations
associated with the subsidy. Net periodic benefit cost for the postretirement
medical plans included the following components for 2001, 2002, and 2003 (in
millions):

<P align="left" style="margin-left: 0%; text-indent: 0%; margin-right: 0%; font-size: 10pt">The fair value of cash equivalents approximates carrying value due to the
short maturity of these instruments. The fair value of marketable securities
is based on quoted market prices. The fair value of fuel hedge contracts is
based on commodity exchange prices. The fair value of restricted deposits
approximates the carrying amount. The fair value of long-term debt is based
on a discounted cash flow analysis using the Company&#146;s current borrowing rate.

<P align="left" style="margin-left: 0%; text-indent: 0%; margin-right: 0%; font-size: 10pt">Diluted EPS is calculated by dividing net income by the average common shares
outstanding plus additional common shares that would have been outstanding
assuming the exercise of in-the-money stock options, using the treasury stock
method. Stock options excluded from the calculation of


<P align="center" style="font-size: 10pt">74
</DIV>

<P align="left" style="margin-left: 0%; text-indent: 0%; margin-right: 0%; font-size: 10pt">diluted EPS because they
are antidilutive, represented 2.9&nbsp;million, 3.3&nbsp;million, and 3.0
million shares, respectively, in 2001, 2002, and 2003.


<P align="left" style="margin-left: 0%; text-indent: 0%; margin-right: 0%; font-size: 10pt">Diluted shares also excludes the shares of common stock issuable upon
conversion of the convertible notes issued on March&nbsp;21, 2003. Although the
Company previously reported its expectation that the notes would become
convertible in the fourth quarter of 2003, the actual closing prices of Air
Group&#146;s common stock during the fourth quarter did not trigger the
convertibility feature.


<P align="left" style="margin-left: 0%; text-indent: 0%; margin-right: 0%; font-size: 10pt"><B>Note 11. Operating Segment Information</B>


<P align="left" style="margin-left: 0%; text-indent: 0%; margin-right: 0%; font-size: 10pt">SFAS No.&nbsp;131, &#147;Disclosures about Segments of an Enterprise and Related
Information&#148;, as amended (SFAS 131), requires that a public company report
annual and interim financial and descriptive information about its reportable
operating segments. Operating segments, as defined, are components of an
enterprise about which separate financial information is available that is
evaluated regularly by the chief operating decision maker in deciding how to
allocate resources and in assessing performance. The Company has two primary
operating and reporting segments, consisting of Alaska and Horizon. These
segments are more fully described in Note 1 under Nature of Operations.

<P align="left" style="margin-left: 0%; text-indent: 0%; margin-right: 0%; font-size: 10pt"><B>Note 12. Special Charge</B>


<P align="left" style="margin-left: 0%; text-indent: 0%; margin-right: 0%; font-size: 10pt">In December&nbsp;2001, Horizon recorded a $10.2&nbsp;million special charge to recognize
the loss in value of its owned Fokker F-28 aircraft engines and related spare
parts, which had a net book value of $16.2&nbsp;million, net of the impairment
charge, at December&nbsp;31, 2001, and $8.1&nbsp;million at December&nbsp;31, 2002. The
F-28s, have been replaced with more fuel-efficient CRJ 700 regional jets, and
were phased completely out of service on February&nbsp;14, 2003.


<P align="center" style="font-size: 10pt">75
</DIV>

<P><HR noshade><P>
<H5 align="left" style="page-break-before:always"><A HREF="#tocpage">Table of Contents</A></H5><P>

<DIV style="font-family: 'Times New Roman',Times,serif">




<P align="left" style="margin-left: 0%; text-indent: 0%; margin-right: 0%; font-size: 10pt"><B>Note 13. U.S. Government Compensation</B>


<P align="left" style="margin-left: 0%; text-indent: 0%; margin-right: 0%; font-size: 10pt">In September&nbsp;2001, the U.S. Government passed the Air Transportation Safety and
System Stabilization Act to provide $5&nbsp;billion of cash compensation and $10
billion of loan guarantees to U.S. airlines. The purpose of this Act was to
compensate the airlines for direct and incremental losses for the period
September&nbsp;11 through December&nbsp;31, 2001 as a result of the September&nbsp;11
terrorist attacks.


<P align="left" style="margin-left: 0%; text-indent: 0%; margin-right: 0%; font-size: 10pt">In 2001, Alaska and Horizon recorded government compensation of $71.6&nbsp;million
and $9.8&nbsp;million, respectively. These amounts are reflected in nonoperating
income (expense)&nbsp;in the consolidated statements of operations. During the
third quarter of 2002, the Department of Transportation completed its review
procedures and remitted final compensation payments to Alaska and Horizon of
$0.2&nbsp;million each.


<P align="left" style="margin-left: 0%; text-indent: 0%; margin-right: 0%; font-size: 10pt">On April&nbsp;16, 2003, the Emergency Wartime Supplemental Appropriations Act (the
Act) was signed into legislation. The Act includes $2.3&nbsp;billion of one-time
cash payments to air carriers, allocated based on each carrier&#146;s share of
security fees remitted and carrier fees paid to the Transportation Security
Administration (TSA)&nbsp;since its inception in February&nbsp;2002. Additionally,
passenger security fees were not imposed by the TSA and carrier fees were not
paid during the period June&nbsp;1, 2003 through September&nbsp;30, 2003. In May&nbsp;2003,
the Company received its share of the one-time cash grant in the amount of
$71.4&nbsp;million ($52.8&nbsp;million for Alaska and $18.6&nbsp;million for Horizon).


<P align="left" style="margin-left: 0%; text-indent: 0%; margin-right: 0%; font-size: 10pt">In August&nbsp;2003, the Company received $2.7&nbsp;million ($2.5&nbsp;million for Alaska and
$0.2&nbsp;million for Horizon) from the Federal Aviation Administration in
reimbursement of flight deck reinforcement expenditures. The reimbursement was
recorded as an offset to capital costs.


<P align="left" style="margin-left: 0%; text-indent: 0%; margin-right: 0%; font-size: 10pt"><B>Note 14. Contingencies</B>


<P align="left" style="margin-left: 0%; text-indent: 0%; margin-right: 0%; font-size: 10pt">The Company is a party to routine litigation incidental to its business and
with respect to which no material liability is expected. Management believes
the ultimate disposition of these matters is not likely to materially affect
the Company&#146;s financial position or results of operations. However, this
belief is based on management&#146;s current understanding of the relevant law and
facts; it is subject to various contingencies, including the potential costs
and risks associated with litigation and the actions of judges and juries.


<P align="left" style="margin-left: 0%; text-indent: 0%; margin-right: 0%; font-size: 10pt"><B>Note 15. Change in Accounting Principle</B>


<P align="left" style="margin-left: 0%; text-indent: 0%; margin-right: 0%; font-size: 10pt">In June&nbsp;2001, the FASB issued SFAS No.&nbsp;142, &#147;Goodwill and Other Intangible
Assets.&#148; Under this statement, goodwill is considered to have an indefinite
life and is no longer amortized but instead is subject to periodic impairment
testing. Effective January&nbsp;1, 2002, the Company adopted SFAS No.&nbsp;142.
Assuming the Company had adopted this standard as of January&nbsp;1, 2001, the
Company&#146;s net loss for the year ended December&nbsp;31, 2001 would have been reduced
by approximately $2.0&nbsp;million ($.08 per share) for the impact of goodwill
amortization.


<P align="left" style="margin-left: 0%; text-indent: 0%; margin-right: 0%; font-size: 10pt">During the second quarter of 2002, the Company completed the first step of its
impairment test related to its $51.4&nbsp;million of goodwill and determined that
the net book value exceeded its fair value. In the fourth quarter of 2002, the
Company completed the second step of its impairment test and determined that
all of the Company&#146;s goodwill was impaired. As a result, the Company recorded
a one-time, non-cash charge, effective January&nbsp;1, 2002 of $51.4&nbsp;million ($12.5
million Alaska and $38.9

<P align="center" style="font-size: 10pt">76
</DIV>

<!-- PAGEBREAK -->
<P><HR noshade><P>
<H5 align="left" style="page-break-before:always"><A HREF="#tocpage">Table of Contents</A></H5><P>

<DIV style="font-family: 'Times New Roman',Times,serif">

<P align="left" style="margin-left: 0%; text-indent: 0%; margin-right: 0%; font-size: 10pt">million Horizon) to write-off all of its goodwill.
This charge is reflected as a cumulative effect of accounting change in the
2002 consolidated statement of operations.



<P align="left" style="margin-left: 0%; text-indent: 0%; margin-right: 0%; font-size: 10pt"><B>Note 16. Horizon Partnership Agreement</B>


<P align="left" style="margin-left: 0%; text-indent: 0%; margin-right: 0%; font-size: 10pt">On January&nbsp;1, 2004, Horizon began operating regional jet service branded as
Frontier JetExpress under a 12-year agreement with Frontier Airlines. Horizon
is currently operating four 70-seat Bombardier CRJ-700 aircraft as Frontier
JetExpress and plans on expanding this fleet to nine airplanes by May&nbsp;30, 2004.
Horizon is responsible for flying and maintaining the aircraft and Frontier
maintains control over scheduling and destinations. In exchange for providing
these services, Horizon receives a base fee and performance-based incentives.

<P align="center" style="font-size: 10pt"><B>INDEPENDENT AUDITORS&#146; REPORT</B>



<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">To the Board of Directors and Shareholders of Alaska Air Group, Inc.<br>
Seattle, Washington



<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;We have audited the accompanying consolidated balance sheets of Alaska Air
Group, Inc. (a Delaware corporation) and subsidiaries (the Company) as of
December&nbsp;31, 2003 and 2002, and the related consolidated statements of
operations, shareholders&#146; equity, and cash flows for each of the three years in
the period ended December&nbsp;31, 2003. Our audits also included the financial
statement schedule listed in the Index at Item&nbsp;15(a). These financial
statements and financial statement schedule are the responsibility of the
Company&#146;s management. Our responsibility is to express an opinion on these
financial statements based on our audits.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;We conducted our audits in accordance with auditing standards generally
accepted in the United States of America. Those standards require that we plan
and perform the audit to obtain reasonable assurance about whether the
financial statements are free of material misstatement. An audit includes
examining, on a test basis, evidence supporting the amounts and disclosures in
the financial statements. An audit also includes assessing the accounting
principles used and significant estimates made by management, as well as
evaluating the overall financial statement presentation. We believe that our
audits provide a reasonable basis for our opinion.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In our opinion, such consolidated financial statements present fairly, in
all material respects, the financial position of Alaska Air Group, Inc. and
subsidiaries as of December&nbsp;31, 2003 and 2002, and the results of their
operations and their cash flows for each of the three years in the period ended
December&nbsp;31, 2003, in conformity with accounting principles generally accepted
in the United States of America. Also, in our opinion, such financial
statement schedule, when considered in relation to the basic consolidated
financial statements taken as a whole, presents fairly in all material respects
the information set forth therein.


<P align="left" style="font-size: 10pt">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;As discussed in Note 15 to the consolidated financial statements, the
Company adopted Statement of Financial Accounting Standards No.&nbsp;142, <I>Goodwill
and Other Intangible Assets, </I>effective January&nbsp;1, 2002.

                                                                    EXHIBIT 12.1

                       RATIO OF EARNINGS TO FIXED CHARGES

                                                                    EXHIBIT 31.1

                                 CERTIFICATIONS

I, William S. Ayer, certify that:

1.       I have reviewed this annual report on Form 10-K of Alaska Air Group,
         Inc. for the period ended December 31, 2003;

2.       Based on my knowledge, this quarterly report does not contain any
         untrue statement of a material fact or omit to state a material fact
         necessary to make the statements made, in light of the circumstances
         under which such statements were made, not misleading with respect to
         the period covered by this report;

3.       Based on my knowledge, the financial statements, and other financial
         information included in this quarterly report, fairly present in all
         material respects the financial condition, results of operations and
         cash flows of the registrant as of, and for, the periods presented in
         this report;

4.       The registrant's other certifying officer and I are responsible for
         establishing and maintaining disclosure controls and procedures (as
         defined in Exchange Act Rules 13a-15(e) and 15d-15(e)) for the
         registrant and we have:

         a) designed such disclosure controls and procedures, or caused such
         disclosure controls and procedures to be designed under our
         supervision, to ensure that material information relating to the
         registrant, including its consolidated subsidiaries, is made known to
         us by others within those entities, particularly during the period in
         which this report is being prepared;

         b) evaluated the effectiveness of the registrant's disclosure controls
         and procedures and presented in this report our conclusions about the
         effectiveness of the disclosure controls and procedures, as of December
         31, 2003, based on such evaluation; and

         c) disclosed in this report any change in the registrant's internal
         control over financial reporting that occurred during the registrant's
         most recent fiscal quarter (the registrant's fourth fiscal quarter in
         the case of an annual report) that has materially affected, or is
         reasonably likely to materially affect, the registrant's internal
         control over financial reporting; and

5.       The registrant's other certifying officers and I have disclosed, based
         on our most recent evaluation of internal control over financial
         reporting, to the registrant's auditors and the audit committee of the
         registrant's board of directors (or persons performing the equivalent
         function):

         a) all significant deficiencies and material weaknesses in the design
         or operation of internal control over financial reporting which are
         reasonably likely to adversely affect the registrant's ability to
         record, process, summarize and report financial information; and

         b) any fraud, whether or not material, that involves management or
         other employees who have a significant role in the registrant's
         internal control over financial reporting.

         March 10, 2004                                By /s/ William S. Ayer
                                                          ----------------------
                                                       William S. Ayer
                                                       Chairman, President & CEO
